<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Emmy Noether</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">German Jewish mathematician (1882–1935)</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output <p><b>Amalie Emmy Noether</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rufname_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rufname-1">[a]</a></sup> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">US</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ʌ/: 'u' in 'cut'">ʌ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ər/: 'er' in 'letter'">ər</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">UK</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ɜː/: r-less 'ur' in 'nurse'">ɜː</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <small>German: </small><span class="IPA" lang="de-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈnøːtɐ]</a></span>; 23 March 1882 – 14 April 1935) was a <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">German</a> <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematician</a> who made many important contributions to <a href="/wiki/Abstract_algebra" title="Abstract algebra">abstract algebra</a>. She discovered Noether's <a href="/wiki/Noether%27s_theorem" title="Noether's theorem">First</a> and <a href="/wiki/Noether%27s_second_theorem" title="Noether's second theorem">Second</a> Theorem, which are fundamental in <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_physics" title="Mathematical physics">mathematical physics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[1]</a></sup> She was described by <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Alexandrov" title="Pavel Alexandrov">Pavel Alexandrov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Dieudonn%C3%A9" title="Jean Dieudonné">Jean Dieudonné</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Weyl" title="Hermann Weyl">Hermann Weyl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Wiener" title="Norbert Wiener">Norbert Wiener</a> as the most important <a href="/wiki/List_of_women_in_mathematics" title="List of women in mathematics">woman in the history of mathematics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-einstein_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-einstein-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexandrov1981100_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexandrov1981100-4">[3]</a></sup> As one of the leading mathematicians of her time, she developed some theories of <a href="/wiki/Ring_(mathematics)" title="Ring (mathematics)">rings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Field_(mathematics)" title="Field (mathematics)">fields</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Algebra_over_a_field" title="Algebra over a field">algebras</a>. In physics, <a href="/wiki/Noether%27s_theorem" title="Noether's theorem">Noether's theorem</a> explains the connection between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symmetry_in_physics" title="Symmetry in physics">symmetry</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_law_(physics)" title="Conservation law (physics)">conservation laws</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neeman_1999_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-neeman_1999-5">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Noether was born to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_family" title="Jewish family">Jewish family</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Franconia" title="Franconia">Franconian</a> town of <a href="/wiki/Erlangen" title="Erlangen">Erlangen</a>; her father was the mathematician <a href="/wiki/Max_Noether" title="Max Noether">Max Noether</a>. She originally planned to teach French and English after passing the required examinations, but instead studied mathematics at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Erlangen" title="University of Erlangen">University of Erlangen</a>, where her father lectured. After completing her doctorate in 1907<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6">[5]</a></sup> under the supervision of <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gordan" title="Paul Gordan">Paul Gordan</a>, she worked at the Mathematical Institute of Erlangen without pay for seven years. At the time, women were largely excluded from academic positions. In 1915, she was invited by <a href="/wiki/David_Hilbert" title="David Hilbert">David Hilbert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Felix_Klein" title="Felix Klein">Felix Klein</a> to join the mathematics department at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_G%C3%B6ttingen" title="University of Göttingen">University of Göttingen</a>, a world-renowned center of mathematical research. The philosophical faculty objected, however, and she spent four years lecturing under Hilbert's name. Her <a href="/wiki/Habilitation" title="Habilitation">habilitation</a> was approved in 1919, allowing her to obtain the rank of <i><a href="/wiki/Privatdozent" title="Privatdozent">Privatdozent</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Noether remained a leading member of the <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen" title="Göttingen">Göttingen</a> mathematics department until 1933; her students were sometimes called the "Noether boys". In 1924, Dutch mathematician <a href="/wiki/Bartel_Leendert_van_der_Waerden" title="Bartel Leendert van der Waerden">B. L. van der Waerden</a> joined her circle and soon became the leading expositor of Noether's ideas; her work was the foundation for the second volume of his influential 1931 textbook, <i><a href="/wiki/Moderne_Algebra" title="Moderne Algebra">Moderne Algebra</a></i>. By the time of her plenary address at the 1932 <a href="/wiki/International_Congress_of_Mathematicians" title="International Congress of Mathematicians">International Congress of Mathematicians</a> in <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a>, her algebraic acumen was recognized around the world. The following year, Germany's Nazi government <a href="/wiki/Anti-Jewish_legislation_in_pre-war_Nazi_Germany" title="Anti-Jewish legislation in pre-war Nazi Germany">dismissed Jews from university positions</a>, and Noether moved to the United States to take up a position at <a href="/wiki/Bryn_Mawr_College" title="Bryn Mawr College">Bryn Mawr College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> where she taught, among others, doctoral and post-graduate women including <a href="/wiki/Marie_Johanna_Weiss" title="Marie Johanna Weiss">Marie Johanna Weiss</a>, Ruth Stauffer, Grace Shover Quinn and <a href="/wiki/Olga_Taussky-Todd" title="Olga Taussky-Todd">Olga Taussky-Todd</a>. At the same time, she lectured and performed research at the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Advanced_Study" title="Institute for Advanced Study">Institute for Advanced Study</a> in <a href="/wiki/Princeton,_New_Jersey" title="Princeton, New Jersey">Princeton, New Jersey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Noether's mathematical work has been divided into three "epochs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weyl_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weyl-7">[6]</a></sup> In the first (1908–1919), she made contributions to the theories of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algebraic_invariant" title="Algebraic invariant">algebraic invariants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Field_(mathematics)" title="Field (mathematics)">number fields</a>. Her work on differential invariants in the <a href="/wiki/Calculus_of_variations" title="Calculus of variations">calculus of variations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noether%27s_theorem" title="Noether's theorem">Noether's theorem</a>, has been called "one of the most important mathematical theorems ever proved in guiding the development of modern physics".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200473_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200473-8">[7]</a></sup> In the second epoch (1920–1926), she began work that "changed the face of [abstract] algebra".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weyl_128_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-weyl_128-9">[8]</a></sup> In her classic 1921 paper <i>Idealtheorie in Ringbereichen</i> (<i>Theory of Ideals in Ring Domains</i>), Noether developed the theory of <a href="/wiki/Ideal_(ring_theory)" title="Ideal (ring theory)">ideals</a> in <a href="/wiki/Commutative_ring" title="Commutative ring">commutative rings</a> into a tool with wide-ranging applications. She made elegant use of the <a href="/wiki/Ascending_chain_condition" title="Ascending chain condition">ascending chain condition</a>, and objects satisfying it are named <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noetherian_(disambiguation)" title="Noetherian (disambiguation)">Noetherian</a></i> in her honor. In the third epoch (1927–1935), she published works on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noncommutative_algebra" title="Noncommutative algebra">noncommutative algebras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hypercomplex_number" title="Hypercomplex number">hypercomplex numbers</a> and united the <a href="/wiki/Representation_theory" title="Representation theory">representation theory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Group_(mathematics)" title="Group (mathematics)">groups</a> with the theory of <a href="/wiki/Module_(mathematics)" title="Module (mathematics)">modules</a> and ideals. In addition to her own publications, Noether was generous with her ideas and is credited with several lines of research published by other mathematicians, even in fields far removed from her main work, such as <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_topology" title="Algebraic topology">algebraic topology</a>.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Erlangen_1916.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1616" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Erlangen_1916.jpg/220px-Erlangen_1916.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Erlangen_1916.jpg/330px-Erlangen_1916.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Erlangen_1916.jpg/440px-Erlangen_1916.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Erlangen_1916.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Noether grew up in the Bavarian city of <a href="/wiki/Erlangen" title="Erlangen">Erlangen</a>, depicted here in a 1916 postcard</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NoetherFamily_MFO3120.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="254" data-file-width="272" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/NoetherFamily_MFO3120.jpg/220px-NoetherFamily_MFO3120.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0b/NoetherFamily_MFO3120.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NoetherFamily_MFO3120.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Emmy Noether with her brothers Alfred, <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Noether" title="Fritz Noether">Fritz</a>, and Robert, before 1918</div></div></div>
<p>Emmy Noether was born on 23 March 1882, the first of four children of mathematician <a href="/wiki/Max_Noether" title="Max Noether">Max Noether</a> and Ida Amalia Kaufmann, both from Jewish merchant families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> Her first name was "Amalie", after her mother and paternal grandmother, but she began using her middle name at a young age, and she invariably used the name "Emmy Noether" in her adult life and her publications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rufname_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rufname-1">[a]</a></sup>
</p><p>In her youth, Noether did not stand out academically although she was known for being clever and friendly. She was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-sighted" title="Near-sighted">near-sighted</a> and talked with a minor <a href="/wiki/Lisp" title="Lisp">lisp</a> during her childhood. A family friend recounted a story years later about young Noether quickly solving a brain teaser at a children's party, showing logical acumen at that early age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick19819–10_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick19819–10-11">[10]</a></sup> She was taught to cook and clean, as were most girls of the time, and she took piano lessons. She pursued none of these activities with passion, although she loved to dance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198110–11_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198110–11-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>She had three younger brothers: the eldest, Alfred, was born in 1883, was awarded a doctorate in <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> from Erlangen in 1909, but died nine years later. <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Noether" title="Fritz Noether">Fritz Noether</a>, born in 1884, is remembered for his academic accomplishments. After studying in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> he made a reputation for himself in <a href="/wiki/Applied_mathematics" title="Applied mathematics">applied mathematics</a>. He was executed in the Soviet Union in 1941. The youngest, Gustav Robert, was born in 1889. Very little is known about his life; he suffered from chronic illness and died in 1928.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198125,_45_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198125,_45-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling5_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling5-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1935, Noether underwent surgery for an <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_cyst" title="Ovarian cyst">ovarian cyst</a> and, despite signs of a recovery, died four days later at the age of 53.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="University_life_and_education">University life and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: University life and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paul_Albert_Gordan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="282" data-file-width="311" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Paul_Albert_Gordan.jpg/220px-Paul_Albert_Gordan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/07/Paul_Albert_Gordan.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paul_Albert_Gordan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Paul_Gordan" title="Paul Gordan">Paul Gordan</a> supervised Noether's doctoral dissertation on <a href="/wiki/Invariant_(mathematics)" title="Invariant (mathematics)">invariants</a> of biquadratic forms.</div></div></div>
<p>Noether showed early proficiency in French and English. In the spring of 1900, she took the examination for teachers of these languages and received an overall score of <i>sehr gut</i> (very good). Her performance qualified her to teach languages at schools reserved for girls, but she chose instead to continue her studies at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Erlangen" title="University of Erlangen">University of Erlangen</a>.
</p><p>This was an unconventional decision; two years earlier, the Academic Senate of the university had declared that allowing <a href="/wiki/Mixed-sex_education" title="Mixed-sex education">mixed-sex education</a> would "overthrow all academic order".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198110_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198110-15">[14]</a></sup> One of only two women in a university of 986 students, Noether was allowed only to <a href="/wiki/Academic_audit" title="Academic audit">audit</a> classes rather than participate fully, and required the permission of individual professors whose lectures she wished to attend. Despite these obstacles, on 14 July 1903 she passed the graduation exam at a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Realgymnasium" title="Realgymnasium">Realgymnasium</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198111–12_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198111–12-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling19818–10_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling19818–10-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200471_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200471-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 1903–1904 winter semester, she studied at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_G%C3%B6ttingen" title="University of Göttingen">University of Göttingen</a>, attending lectures given by astronomer <a href="/wiki/Karl_Schwarzschild" title="Karl Schwarzschild">Karl Schwarzschild</a> and mathematicians <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Minkowski" title="Hermann Minkowski">Hermann Minkowski</a>, <a href="/wiki/Otto_Blumenthal" title="Otto Blumenthal">Otto Blumenthal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Felix_Klein" title="Felix Klein">Felix Klein</a>, and <a href="/wiki/David_Hilbert" title="David Hilbert">David Hilbert</a>. Soon thereafter, restrictions on women's participation in that university were rescinded.
</p><p>Noether returned to Erlangen. She officially reentered the university in October 1904, and declared her intention to focus solely on mathematics. Under the supervision of <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gordan" title="Paul Gordan">Paul Gordan</a> she wrote her dissertation, <i>Über die Bildung des Formensystems der ternären biquadratischen Form</i> (<i>On Complete Systems of Invariants for Ternary Biquadratic Forms</i>, 1907). Gordan was a member of the "computational" school of invariant researchers, and Noether's thesis ended with a list of over 300 explicitly worked out invariants. This approach to invariants was later superseded by the more abstract and general approach pioneered by Hilbert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMerzbach1983164_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMerzbach1983164-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198110–11_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198110–11-20">[19]</a></sup> Although it had been well received, Noether later described her thesis and a number of subsequent similar papers she produced as "crap".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198110–11_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198110–11-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198113–17_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198113–17-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[b]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Teaching_period">Teaching period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Teaching period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="University_of_Erlangen">University of Erlangen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: University of Erlangen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For the next seven years (1908–1915) she taught at the University of Erlangen's Mathematical Institute without pay, occasionally substituting for her father when he was too ill to lecture. In 1910 and 1911 she published an extension of her thesis work from three variables to <i>n</i> variables.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emmy_noether_postcard_1915.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1789" data-file-width="1403" decoding="async" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Emmy_noether_postcard_1915.jpg/250px-Emmy_noether_postcard_1915.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Emmy_noether_postcard_1915.jpg/375px-Emmy_noether_postcard_1915.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Emmy_noether_postcard_1915.jpg/500px-Emmy_noether_postcard_1915.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emmy_noether_postcard_1915.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Noether sometimes used postcards to discuss abstract algebra with her colleague, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Sigismund_Fischer" title="Ernst Sigismund Fischer">Ernst Fischer</a>. This card is postmarked 10 April 1915.</div></div></div>
<p>Gordan retired in the spring of 1910, but continued to teach occasionally with his successor, <a href="/wiki/Erhard_Schmidt" title="Erhard Schmidt">Erhard Schmidt</a>, who left shortly afterward for a position in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breslau" title="Breslau">Breslau</a>. Gordan retired from teaching altogether in 1911 when Schmidt's successor <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Sigismund_Fischer" title="Ernst Sigismund Fischer">Ernst Fischer</a> arrived; Gordan died a year later in December 1912.
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Weyl" title="Hermann Weyl">Hermann Weyl</a>, Fischer was an important influence on Noether, in particular by introducing her to the work of <a href="/wiki/David_Hilbert" title="David Hilbert">David Hilbert</a>. From 1913 to 1916 Noether published several papers extending and applying Hilbert's methods to mathematical objects such as <a href="/wiki/Field_(mathematics)" title="Field (mathematics)">fields</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rational_function" title="Rational function">rational functions</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Invariant_theory" title="Invariant theory">invariants</a> of <a href="/wiki/Finite_group" title="Finite group">finite groups</a>. This phase marks the beginning of her engagement with <a href="/wiki/Abstract_algebra" title="Abstract algebra">abstract algebra</a>, the field of mathematics to which she would make groundbreaking contributions.
</p><p>Noether and Fischer shared lively enjoyment of mathematics and would often discuss lectures long after they were over; Noether is known to have sent postcards to Fischer continuing her train of mathematical thoughts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198111–12_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198111–12-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198118–24_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198118–24-24">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="University_of_G.C3.B6ttingen"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="University_of_Göttingen">University of Göttingen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: University of Göttingen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the spring of 1915, Noether was invited to return to the University of Göttingen by David Hilbert and <a href="/wiki/Felix_Klein" title="Felix Klein">Felix Klein</a>. Their effort to recruit her, however, was blocked by the <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philologists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Historian" title="Historian">historians</a> among the philosophical faculty: Women, they insisted, should not become <i><a href="/wiki/Privatdozent" title="Privatdozent">privatdozenten</a></i>. One faculty member protested: "<i>What will our soldiers think when they return to the university and find that they are required to learn at the feet of a woman?</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198114_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198114-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198132_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198132-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200472_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200472-27">[25]</a></sup> Hilbert responded with indignation, stating, "<i>I do not see that the sex of the candidate is an argument against her admission as privatdozent. After all, we are a university, not a bathhouse.</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198114_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198114-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198132_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198132-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200472_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200472-27">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hilbert.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="592" data-file-width="437" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Hilbert.jpg/170px-Hilbert.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Hilbert.jpg/255px-Hilbert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Hilbert.jpg/340px-Hilbert.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hilbert.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1915 <a href="/wiki/David_Hilbert" title="David Hilbert">David Hilbert</a> invited Noether to join the Göttingen mathematics department, challenging the views of some of his colleagues that a woman should not be allowed to teach at a university.</div></div></div>
<p>Noether left for Göttingen in late April; two weeks later her mother died suddenly in Erlangen. She had previously received medical care for an eye condition, but its nature and impact on her death is unknown. At about the same time Noether's father retired and her brother joined the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Army_(German_Empire)" title="German Army (German Empire)">German Army</a> to serve in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. She returned to Erlangen for several weeks, mostly to care for her aging father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198124–26_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198124–26-28">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>During her first years teaching at Göttingen she did not have an official position and was not paid; her family paid for her room and board and supported her academic work. Her lectures often were advertised under Hilbert's name, and Noether would provide "assistance".
</p><p>Soon after arriving at Göttingen, however, she demonstrated her capabilities by proving the <a href="/wiki/Theorem" title="Theorem">theorem</a> now known as <a href="/wiki/Noether%27s_theorem" title="Noether's theorem">Noether's theorem</a>, which shows that a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_law_(physics)" title="Conservation law (physics)">conservation law</a> is associated with any <a href="/wiki/Derivative" title="Derivative">differentiable</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symmetry_in_physics" title="Symmetry in physics">symmetry of a physical system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200472_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200472-27">[25]</a></sup> The paper was presented by a colleague, F. Klein, on 26 July 1918 to a meeting of the Royal Society of Sciences at Göttingen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoether1918c235_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoether1918c235-29">[27]</a></sup> Noether presumably did not present it herself because she was not a member of the society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEByers19962_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEByers19962-30">[28]</a></sup> American physicists <a href="/wiki/Leon_M._Lederman" title="Leon M. Lederman">Leon M. Lederman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_T._Hill" title="Christopher T. Hill">Christopher T. Hill</a> argue in their book <i>Symmetry and the Beautiful Universe</i> that Noether's theorem is "certainly one of the most important mathematical theorems ever proved in guiding the development of <a href="/wiki/Modern_physics" title="Modern physics">modern physics</a>, possibly on a par with the <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_theorem" title="Pythagorean theorem">Pythagorean theorem</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200473_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200473-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mathematik_G%C3%B6ttingen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="3612" decoding="async" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Mathematik_G%C3%B6ttingen.jpg/210px-Mathematik_G%C3%B6ttingen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Mathematik_G%C3%B6ttingen.jpg/315px-Mathematik_G%C3%B6ttingen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Mathematik_G%C3%B6ttingen.jpg/420px-Mathematik_G%C3%B6ttingen.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mathematik_G%C3%B6ttingen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The mathematics department at the University of Göttingen allowed Noether's <i><a href="/wiki/Habilitation" title="Habilitation">habilitation</a></i> in 1919, four years after she had begun lecturing at the school.</div></div></div>
<p>When World War I ended, the <a href="/wiki/German_Revolution_of_1918%E2%80%931919" title="German Revolution of 1918–1919">German Revolution of 1918–1919</a> brought a significant change in social attitudes, including more rights for women. In 1919 the University of Göttingen allowed Noether to proceed with her <i><a href="/wiki/Habilitation" title="Habilitation">habilitation</a></i> (eligibility for tenure). Her oral examination was held in late May, and she successfully delivered her <i>habilitation</i> lecture in June 1919.
</p><p>Three years later she received a letter from <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Otto_Boelitz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Otto Boelitz (page does not exist)">Otto Boelitz</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Otto_Boelitz" title="de:Otto Boelitz">de</a>]</span>, the <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussian</a> Minister for Science, Art, and Public Education, in which he conferred on her the title of <i>nicht beamteter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ausserordentlicher_Professor" title="Ausserordentlicher Professor">ausserordentlicher Professor</a></i> (an untenured professor with limited internal administrative rights and functions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick1981188_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick1981188-31">[29]</a></sup>). This was an unpaid "extraordinary" <a href="/wiki/Professor" title="Professor">professorship</a>, not the higher "ordinary" professorship, which was a civil-service position. Although it recognized the importance of her work, the position still provided no salary. Noether was not paid for her lectures until she was appointed to the special position of <i>Lehrbeauftragte für Algebra</i> a year later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198114–18_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198114–18-32">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198133–34_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198133–34-33">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_in_abstract_algebra">Work in abstract algebra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Work in abstract algebra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2><p>
Although Noether's theorem had a significant effect upon classical and quantum mechanics, among mathematicians she is best remembered for her contributions to <a href="/wiki/Abstract_algebra" title="Abstract algebra">abstract algebra</a>. In his introduction to Noether's <i>Collected Papers</i>, <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Jacobson" title="Nathan Jacobson">Nathan Jacobson</a> wrote that</p><blockquote><p>The development of abstract algebra, which is one of the most distinctive innovations of twentieth century mathematics, is largely due to her – in published papers, in lectures, and in personal influence on her contemporaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoether1983_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoether1983-34">[32]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p> She sometimes allowed her colleagues and students to receive credit for her ideas, helping them develop their careers at the expense of her own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200474_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200474-35">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Noether's work in algebra began in 1920. In collaboration with W. Schmeidler, she then published a paper about the <a href="/wiki/Ideal_theory" title="Ideal theory">theory of ideals</a> in which they defined <a href="/wiki/Ideal_(ring_theory)" title="Ideal (ring theory)">left and right ideals</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Ring_(mathematics)" title="Ring (mathematics)">ring</a>.
</p><p>The following year she published a paper called <i>Idealtheorie in Ringbereichen</i>, analyzing <a href="/wiki/Ascending_chain_condition" title="Ascending chain condition">ascending chain conditions</a> with regard to (mathematical) <a href="/wiki/Ideal_(ring_theory)" title="Ideal (ring theory)">ideals</a>. Noted algebraist <a href="/wiki/Irving_Kaplansky" title="Irving Kaplansky">Irving Kaplansky</a> called this work "revolutionary";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198118_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198118-36">[34]</a></sup> the publication gave rise to the term "<a href="/wiki/Noetherian_ring" title="Noetherian ring">Noetherian ring</a>" and the naming of several other mathematical objects as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noetherian_(disambiguation)" title="Noetherian (disambiguation)">Noetherian</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198118_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198118-36">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198144–45_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198144–45-37">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1924 a young Dutch mathematician, <a href="/wiki/Bartel_Leendert_van_der_Waerden" title="Bartel Leendert van der Waerden">B.L. van der Waerden</a>, arrived at the University of Göttingen. He immediately began working with Noether, who provided invaluable methods of abstract conceptualization. Van der Waerden later said that her originality was "absolute beyond comparison".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden1935100_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden1935100-38">[36]</a></sup> In 1931 he published <i>Moderne Algebra</i>, a central text in the field; its second volume borrowed heavily from Noether's work. Although Noether did not seek recognition, he included as a note in the seventh edition "based in part on lectures by <a href="/wiki/Emil_Artin" title="Emil Artin">E. Artin</a> and E. Noether".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198157–58_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198157–58-39">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198119_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198119-40">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200474_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELedermanHill200474-35">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Van der Waerden's visit was part of a convergence of mathematicians from all over the world to Göttingen, which became a major hub of mathematical and physical research. From 1926 to 1930 Russian <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Alexandrov" title="Pavel Alexandrov">Pavel Alexandrov</a> lectured at the university, and he and Noether quickly became good friends. He began referring to her as <i>der Noether</i>, using the masculine German article as a term of endearment to show his respect. She tried to arrange for him to obtain a position at Göttingen as a regular professor, but was able only to help him secure a scholarship from the <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Foundation" title="Rockefeller Foundation">Rockefeller Foundation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198124–25_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198124–25-41">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198161–63_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198161–63-42">[40]</a></sup> They met regularly and enjoyed discussions about the intersections of algebra and topology. In his 1935 memorial address, Alexandrov named Emmy Noether "the greatest woman mathematician of all time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexandrov1981100,_107_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexandrov1981100,_107-43">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Graduate_students_and_influential_lectures">Graduate students and influential lectures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Graduate students and influential lectures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2><p>
In addition to her mathematical insight, Noether was respected for her consideration of others. Although she sometimes acted rudely toward those who disagreed with her, she nevertheless gained a reputation for constant helpfulness and patient guidance of new students. Her loyalty to mathematical precision caused one colleague to name her "a severe critic", but she combined this demand for accuracy with a nurturing attitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198137–49_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198137–49-44">[42]</a></sup> A colleague later described her this way:</p><blockquote><p>Completely unegotistical and free of vanity, she never claimed anything for herself, but promoted the works of her students above all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden193598_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden193598-45">[43]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span id="G.C3.B6ttingen"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Göttingen">Göttingen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Göttingen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EmmyNoether_MFO3096.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="862" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/EmmyNoether_MFO3096.jpg/220px-EmmyNoether_MFO3096.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/EmmyNoether_MFO3096.jpg/330px-EmmyNoether_MFO3096.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/EmmyNoether_MFO3096.jpg/440px-EmmyNoether_MFO3096.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EmmyNoether_MFO3096.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Noether c. 1930</div></div></div>
<p>In Göttingen, Noether supervised more than a dozen doctoral students; her first was <a href="/wiki/Grete_Hermann" title="Grete Hermann">Grete Hermann</a>, who defended her dissertation in February 1925. She later spoke reverently of her "dissertation-mother".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198151_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198151-46">[44]</a></sup> Noether also supervised <a href="/wiki/Max_Deuring" title="Max Deuring">Max Deuring</a>, who distinguished himself as an undergraduate and went on to contribute to the field of <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic_geometry" title="Arithmetic geometry">arithmetic geometry</a>; <a href="/wiki/Hans_Fitting" title="Hans Fitting">Hans Fitting</a>, remembered for <a href="/wiki/Fitting%27s_theorem" title="Fitting's theorem">Fitting's theorem</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Fitting_lemma" title="Fitting lemma">Fitting lemma</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Chiungtze_C._Tsen" title="Chiungtze C. Tsen">Zeng Jiongzhi</a> (also rendered "Chiungtze C. Tsen" in English), who proved <a href="/wiki/Tsen%27s_theorem" title="Tsen's theorem">Tsen's theorem</a>. She also worked closely with <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Krull" title="Wolfgang Krull">Wolfgang Krull</a>, who greatly advanced <a href="/wiki/Commutative_algebra" title="Commutative algebra">commutative algebra</a> with his <a href="/wiki/Krull%27s_principal_ideal_theorem" title="Krull's principal ideal theorem"><i>Hauptidealsatz</i></a> and his <a href="/wiki/Krull_dimension" title="Krull dimension">dimension theory</a> for commutative rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198153–57_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198153–57-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Her frugal lifestyle at first was due to her being denied pay for her work; however, even after the university began paying her a small salary in 1923, she continued to live a simple and modest life. She was paid more generously later in her life, but saved half of her salary to bequeath to her nephew, <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_E._Noether" title="Gottfried E. Noether">Gottfried E. Noether</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198146–48_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198146–48-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biographers suggest that she was mostly unconcerned about appearance and manners, focusing on her studies. A distinguished algebraist <a href="/wiki/Olga_Taussky-Todd" title="Olga Taussky-Todd">Olga Taussky-Todd</a> described a luncheon during which Noether, wholly engrossed in a discussion of mathematics, "gesticulated wildly" as she ate and "spilled her food constantly and wiped it off from her dress, completely unperturbed".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaussky198180_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaussky198180-49">[47]</a></sup> Appearance-conscious students cringed as she retrieved the handkerchief from her blouse and ignored the increasing disarray of her hair during a lecture. Two female students once approached her during a break in a two-hour class to express their concern, but they were unable to break through the energetic mathematical discussion she was having with other students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198140–41_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198140–41-50">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to van der Waerden's obituary of Emmy Noether, she did not follow a lesson plan for her lectures, which frustrated some students. Instead, she used her lectures as a spontaneous discussion time with her students, to think through and clarify important problems in mathematics. Some of her most important results were developed in these lectures, and the lecture notes of her students formed the basis for several important textbooks, such as those of van der Waerden and Deuring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden1935_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden1935-51">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several of her colleagues attended her lectures, and she allowed some of her ideas, such as the <a href="/wiki/Crossed_product" title="Crossed product">crossed product</a> (<i>verschränktes Produkt</i> in German) of associative algebras, to be published by others. Noether was recorded as having given at least five semester-long courses at Göttingen:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scharlau_49_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-scharlau_49-52">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Winter 1924/1925: <i>Gruppentheorie und hyperkomplexe Zahlen</i> [<i>Group Theory and Hypercomplex Numbers</i>]</li>
<li>Winter 1927/1928: <i>Hyperkomplexe Grössen und Darstellungstheorie</i> [<i>Hypercomplex Quantities and Representation Theory</i>]</li>
<li>Summer 1928: <i>Nichtkommutative Algebra</i> [<i>Noncommutative Algebra</i>]</li>
<li>Summer 1929: <i>Nichtkommutative Arithmetik</i> [<i>Noncommutative Arithmetic</i>]</li>
<li>Winter 1929/30: <i>Algebra der hyperkomplexen Grössen</i> [<i>Algebra of Hypercomplex Quantities</i>]</li></ul>
<p>These courses often preceded major publications on the same subjects.
</p><p>Noether spoke quickly – reflecting the speed of her thoughts, many said – and demanded great concentration from her students. Students who disliked her style often felt alienated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMac_Lane198177_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMac_Lane198177-53">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198137_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198137-54">[52]</a></sup> Some pupils felt that she relied too much on spontaneous discussions. Her most dedicated students, however, relished the enthusiasm with which she approached mathematics, especially since her lectures often built on earlier work they had done together.
</p><p>She developed a close circle of colleagues and students who thought along similar lines and tended to exclude those who did not. "Outsiders" who occasionally visited Noether's lectures usually spent only 30 minutes in the room before leaving in frustration or confusion. A regular student said of one such instance: "The enemy has been defeated; he has cleared out."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198138–41_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198138–41-55">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Noether showed a devotion to her subject and her students that extended beyond the academic day. Once, when the building was closed for a state holiday, she gathered the class on the steps outside, led them through the woods, and lectured at a local coffee house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMac_Lane198171_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMac_Lane198171-56">[54]</a></sup> Later, after <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> dismissed her from teaching, she invited students into her home to discuss their plans for the future and mathematical concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198176_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198176-57">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moscow">Moscow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Moscow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paul_S_Alexandroff_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="273" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Paul_S_Alexandroff_2.jpg/140px-Paul_S_Alexandroff_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Paul_S_Alexandroff_2.jpg/210px-Paul_S_Alexandroff_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b2/Paul_S_Alexandroff_2.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paul_S_Alexandroff_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pavel_Alexandrov" title="Pavel Alexandrov">Pavel Alexandrov</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the winter of 1928–1929 Noether accepted an invitation to <a href="/wiki/Moscow_State_University" title="Moscow State University">Moscow State University</a>, where she continued working with <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Alexandrov" title="Pavel Alexandrov">P.S. Alexandrov</a>. In addition to carrying on with her research, she taught classes in abstract algebra and <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_geometry" title="Algebraic geometry">algebraic geometry</a>. She worked with the topologists <a href="/wiki/Lev_Pontryagin" title="Lev Pontryagin">Lev Pontryagin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Chebotaryov" title="Nikolai Chebotaryov">Nikolai Chebotaryov</a>, who later praised her contributions to the development of <a href="/wiki/Galois_theory" title="Galois theory">Galois theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198163–64_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198163–64-58">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198126_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198126-59">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexandrov1981108–10_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexandrov1981108–10-60">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moscow_05-2012_Mokhovaya_05.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2117" data-file-width="4226" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Moscow_05-2012_Mokhovaya_05.jpg/220px-Moscow_05-2012_Mokhovaya_05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Moscow_05-2012_Mokhovaya_05.jpg/330px-Moscow_05-2012_Mokhovaya_05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Moscow_05-2012_Mokhovaya_05.jpg/440px-Moscow_05-2012_Mokhovaya_05.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moscow_05-2012_Mokhovaya_05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Noether taught at the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_State_University" title="Moscow State University">Moscow State University</a> during the winter of 1928–1929.</div></div></div>
<p>Although politics was not central to her life, Noether took a keen interest in political matters and, according to Alexandrov, showed considerable support for the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a>. She was especially happy to see <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> advances in the fields of science and mathematics, which she considered indicative of new opportunities made possible by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolshevik" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> project. This attitude caused her problems in Germany, culminating in her eviction from a <a href="/wiki/Pension_(lodging)" title="Pension (lodging)">pension lodging</a> building, after student leaders complained of living with "a Marxist-leaning Jewess".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexandrov1981106–09_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexandrov1981106–09-61">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Noether planned to return to Moscow, an effort for which she received support from Alexandrov. After she left Germany in 1933 he tried to help her gain a chair at Moscow State University through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narkompros" title="Narkompros">Soviet Education Ministry</a>. Although this effort proved unsuccessful, they corresponded frequently during the 1930s, and in 1935 she made plans for a return to the Soviet Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexandrov1981106–09_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexandrov1981106–09-61">[59]</a></sup> Meanwhile, her brother <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Noether" title="Fritz Noether">Fritz</a> accepted a position at the Research Institute for Mathematics and Mechanics in <a href="/wiki/Tomsk" title="Tomsk">Tomsk</a>, in the Siberian Federal District of Russia, after losing his job in Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198182–83_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198182–83-62">[60]</a></sup> and was subsequently executed during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Purge" title="Great Purge">Great Purge</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition">Recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1932 Emmy Noether and <a href="/wiki/Emil_Artin" title="Emil Artin">Emil Artin</a> received the <a href="/wiki/Ackermann%E2%80%93Teubner_Memorial_Award" title="Ackermann–Teubner Memorial Award">Ackermann–Teubner Memorial Award</a> for their contributions to mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> The prize included a monetary reward of 500 <a href="/wiki/Reichsmark" title="Reichsmark">ℛℳ</a> and was seen as a long-overdue official recognition of her considerable work in the field. Nevertheless, her colleagues expressed frustration at the fact that she was not elected to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Göttingen Academy of Sciences">Göttingen <i>Gesellschaft der Wissenschaften</i></a> (academy of sciences) and was never promoted to the position of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordentlicher_Professor" title="Ordentlicher Professor">Ordentlicher Professor</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198172–73_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198172–73-64">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198126–27_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198126–27-65">[63]</a></sup> (full professor).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick1981188_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick1981188-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zuerich_vier_Kirchen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Zuerich_vier_Kirchen.jpg/220px-Zuerich_vier_Kirchen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Zuerich_vier_Kirchen.jpg/330px-Zuerich_vier_Kirchen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Zuerich_vier_Kirchen.jpg/440px-Zuerich_vier_Kirchen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zuerich_vier_Kirchen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Noether visited <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a> in 1932 to deliver a <a href="/wiki/List_of_International_Congresses_of_Mathematicians_Plenary_and_Invited_Speakers" title="List of International Congresses of Mathematicians Plenary and Invited Speakers">plenary address at the International Congress of Mathematicians</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Noether's colleagues celebrated her fiftieth birthday in 1932, in typical mathematicians' style. <a href="/wiki/Helmut_Hasse" title="Helmut Hasse">Helmut Hasse</a> dedicated an article to her in the <i><a href="/wiki/Mathematische_Annalen" title="Mathematische Annalen">Mathematische Annalen</a></i>, wherein he confirmed her suspicion that some aspects of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noncommutative_algebra" title="Noncommutative algebra">noncommutative algebra</a> are simpler than those of <a href="/wiki/Commutative_algebra" title="Commutative algebra">commutative algebra</a>, by proving a noncommutative <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_reciprocity" title="Quadratic reciprocity">reciprocity law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHasse1933731_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHasse1933731-66">[64]</a></sup> This pleased her immensely. He also sent her a mathematical riddle, which he called the "m<sub>μν</sub>-riddle of syllables". She solved it immediately, but the riddle has been lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198172–73_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198172–73-64">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198126–27_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198126–27-65">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September of the same year, Noether delivered a plenary address (<i>großer Vortrag</i>) on "Hyper-complex systems in their relations to commutative algebra and to number theory" at the <a href="/wiki/International_Congress_of_Mathematicians" title="International Congress of Mathematicians">International Congress of Mathematicians</a> in <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a>. The congress was attended by 800 people, including Noether's colleagues <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Weyl" title="Hermann Weyl">Hermann Weyl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Landau" title="Edmund Landau">Edmund Landau</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Krull" title="Wolfgang Krull">Wolfgang Krull</a>. There were 420 official participants and twenty-one plenary addresses presented. Apparently, Noether's prominent speaking position was a recognition of the importance of her contributions to mathematics. The 1932 congress is sometimes described as the high point of her career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198126–27_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198126–27-65">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198174–75_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198174–75-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Expulsion_from_G.C3.B6ttingen_by_Nazi_Germany"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Expulsion_from_Göttingen_by_Nazi_Germany">Expulsion from Göttingen by Nazi Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Expulsion from Göttingen by Nazi Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> became the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany_(German_Reich)" title="Chancellor of Germany (German Reich)">German <i>Reichskanzler</i></a> in January 1933, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi" title="Nazi">Nazi</a> activity around the country increased dramatically. At the University of Göttingen the German Student Association led the attack on the "un-German spirit" attributed to Jews and was aided by a <i><a href="/wiki/Privatdozent" title="Privatdozent">privatdozent</a></i> named <a href="/wiki/Werner_Weber_(mathematician)" title="Werner Weber (mathematician)">Werner Weber</a>, a former student of Noether. <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">Antisemitic</a> attitudes created a climate hostile to Jewish professors. One young protester reportedly demanded: "Aryan students want <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Mathematik" title="Deutsche Mathematik">Aryan mathematics</a> and not Jewish mathematics."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kim29_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-kim29-68">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the first actions of Hitler's administration was the <a href="/wiki/Law_for_the_Restoration_of_the_Professional_Civil_Service" title="Law for the Restoration of the Professional Civil Service">Law for the Restoration of the Professional Civil Service</a> which removed Jews and politically suspect government employees (including university professors) from their jobs unless they had "demonstrated their loyalty to Germany" by <a href="/wiki/Frontk%C3%A4mpferprivileg" title="Frontkämpferprivileg">serving in World War I</a>. In April 1933 Noether received a notice from the Prussian Ministry for Sciences, Art, and Public Education which read: "On the basis of paragraph 3 of the Civil Service Code of 7 April 1933, I hereby withdraw from you the right to teach at the University of Göttingen."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198175–76_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198175–76-69">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198128–29_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198128–29-70">[68]</a></sup> Several of Noether's colleagues, including <a href="/wiki/Max_Born" title="Max Born">Max Born</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Courant" title="Richard Courant">Richard Courant</a>, also had their positions revoked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198175–76_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198175–76-69">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198128–29_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198128–29-70">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Noether accepted the decision calmly, providing support for others during this difficult time. <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Weyl" title="Hermann Weyl">Hermann Weyl</a> later wrote that "Emmy Noether—her courage, her frankness, her unconcern about her own fate, her conciliatory spirit—was in the midst of all the hatred and meanness, despair and sorrow surrounding us, a moral solace."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kim29_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-kim29-68">[66]</a></sup> Typically, Noether remained focused on mathematics, gathering students in her apartment to discuss <a href="/wiki/Class_field_theory" title="Class field theory">class field theory</a>. When one of her students appeared in the uniform of the Nazi <a href="/wiki/Paramilitary" title="Paramilitary">paramilitary</a> organization <i><a href="/wiki/Sturmabteilung" title="Sturmabteilung">Sturmabteilung</a></i> (SA), she showed no sign of agitation and, reportedly, even laughed about it later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198175–76_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198175–76-69">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198128–29_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198128–29-70">[68]</a></sup> This, however, was before the bloody events of <a href="/wiki/Kristallnacht" title="Kristallnacht">Kristallnacht</a> in 1938, and their praise from Propaganda Minister <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goebbels" title="Joseph Goebbels">Joseph Goebbels</a>.
</p>
<h2><span id="Refuge_at_Bryn_Mawr_and_Princeton.2C_in_the_United_States"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Refuge_at_Bryn_Mawr_and_Princeton,_in_the_United_States">Refuge at Bryn Mawr and Princeton, in the United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Refuge at Bryn Mawr and Princeton, in the United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Entrance_Bryn_Mawr.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2373" data-file-width="3957" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Entrance_Bryn_Mawr.JPG/220px-Entrance_Bryn_Mawr.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Entrance_Bryn_Mawr.JPG/330px-Entrance_Bryn_Mawr.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Entrance_Bryn_Mawr.JPG/440px-Entrance_Bryn_Mawr.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Entrance_Bryn_Mawr.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bryn_Mawr_College" title="Bryn Mawr College">Bryn Mawr College</a> provided a welcoming home for Noether during the last two years of her life.</div></div></div>
<p>As dozens of newly unemployed professors began searching for positions outside of Germany, their colleagues in the United States sought to provide assistance and job opportunities for them. <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Weyl" title="Hermann Weyl">Hermann Weyl</a> were appointed by the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Advanced_Study" title="Institute for Advanced Study">Institute for Advanced Study</a> in <a href="/wiki/Princeton,_New_Jersey" title="Princeton, New Jersey">Princeton</a>, while others worked to find a sponsor required for legal <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a>. Noether was contacted by representatives of two educational institutions: <a href="/wiki/Bryn_Mawr_College" title="Bryn Mawr College">Bryn Mawr College</a>, in the United States, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somerville_College" title="Somerville College">Somerville College</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>, in England. After a series of negotiations with the <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Foundation" title="Rockefeller Foundation">Rockefeller Foundation</a>, a grant to Bryn Mawr was approved for Noether and she took a position there, starting in late 1933.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198178–79_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198178–79-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198130–31_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198130–31-72">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>At Bryn Mawr, Noether met and befriended <a href="/wiki/Anna_Johnson_Pell_Wheeler" title="Anna Johnson Pell Wheeler">Anna Wheeler</a>, who had studied at Göttingen just before Noether arrived there. Another source of support at the college was the Bryn Mawr president, <a href="/wiki/Marion_Edwards_Park" title="Marion Edwards Park">Marion Edwards Park</a>, who enthusiastically invited mathematicians in the area to "see Dr. Noether in action!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198132–33_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198132–33-73">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198180_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198180-74">[72]</a></sup> Noether and a small team of students worked quickly through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_der_Waerden" title="Van der Waerden">van der Waerden</a>'s 1930 book <i>Moderne Algebra I</i> and parts of <a href="/wiki/Erich_Hecke" title="Erich Hecke">Erich Hecke</a>'s <i>Theorie der algebraischen Zahlen</i> (<i>Theory of algebraic numbers</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198180–81_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198180–81-75">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1934, Noether began lecturing at the Institute for Advanced Study in Princeton upon the invitation of <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Flexner" title="Abraham Flexner">Abraham Flexner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Veblen" title="Oswald Veblen">Oswald Veblen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> She also worked with and supervised <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Adrian_Albert" title="Abraham Adrian Albert">Abraham Albert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harry_Vandiver" title="Harry Vandiver">Harry Vandiver</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198181–82_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198181–82-77">[75]</a></sup> However, she remarked about <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a> that she was not welcome at "the men's university, where nothing female is admitted".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198181_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198181-78">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Her time in the United States was pleasant, surrounded as she was by supportive colleagues and absorbed in her favorite subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198183_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198183-79">[77]</a></sup> In the summer of 1934 she briefly returned to Germany to see Emil Artin and her brother <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Noether" title="Fritz Noether">Fritz</a> before he left for Tomsk. Although many of her former colleagues had been forced out of the universities, she was able to use the library as a "foreign scholar".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick198182_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick198182-80">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198134_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198134-81">[79]</a></sup> Without incident, Noether returned to the United States and her studies at Bryn Mawr.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bryn_Mawr_College_Cloisters.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1488" data-file-width="1984" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Bryn_Mawr_College_Cloisters.JPG/250px-Bryn_Mawr_College_Cloisters.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Bryn_Mawr_College_Cloisters.JPG/375px-Bryn_Mawr_College_Cloisters.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Bryn_Mawr_College_Cloisters.JPG/500px-Bryn_Mawr_College_Cloisters.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bryn_Mawr_College_Cloisters.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Noether's ashes were placed under the walkway surrounding the cloisters of Bryn Mawr's <a href="/wiki/Bryn_Mawr_College#M._Carey_Thomas_Library" title="Bryn Mawr College">M. Carey Thomas Library</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In April 1935 doctors discovered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor" title="Tumor">tumor</a> in Noether's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_pelvis" title="Human pelvis">pelvis</a>. Worried about complications from surgery, they ordered two days of bed rest first. During the operation they discovered an <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_cyst" title="Ovarian cyst">ovarian cyst</a> "the size of a large <a href="/wiki/Cantaloupe" title="Cantaloupe">cantaloupe</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198137–38_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198137–38-82">[80]</a></sup> Two smaller tumors in her <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterus</a> appeared to be benign and were not removed, to avoid prolonging surgery. For three days she appeared to convalesce normally, and she recovered quickly from a <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_collapse" title="Circulatory collapse">circulatory collapse</a> on the fourth. On 14 April she fell unconscious, her temperature soared to 109 °F (42.8 °C), and she died. "[I]t is not easy to say what had occurred in Dr. Noether", one of the physicians wrote. "It is possible that there was some form of unusual and virulent infection, which struck the base of the brain where the heat centers are supposed to be located."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198137–38_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198137–38-82">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>A few days after Noether's death her friends and associates at Bryn Mawr held a small memorial service at College President Park's house. Hermann Weyl and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Brauer" title="Richard Brauer">Richard Brauer</a> traveled from Princeton and spoke with Wheeler and Taussky about their departed colleague. In the months that followed, written tributes began to appear around the globe: Albert Einstein<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> joined van der Waerden, Weyl, and <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Alexandrov" title="Pavel Alexandrov">Pavel Alexandrov</a> in paying their respects. Her body was cremated and the ashes interred under the walkway around the cloisters of the <a href="/wiki/Bryn_Mawr_College#M._Carey_Thomas_Library" title="Bryn Mawr College">M. Carey Thomas Library</a> at Bryn Mawr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198139_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198139-84">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contributions_to_mathematics_and_physics">Contributions to mathematics and physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Contributions to mathematics and physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
