<p>Noether's work in <a href="/wiki/Abstract_algebra" title="Abstract algebra">abstract algebra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topology</a> was influential in mathematics, while in physics, <a href="/wiki/Noether%27s_theorem" title="Noether's theorem">Noether's theorem</a> has consequences for <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_system" title="Dynamical system">dynamical systems</a>. She showed an acute propensity for abstract thought, which allowed her to approach problems of mathematics in fresh and original ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198111–12_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198111–12-23">[21]</a></sup> Her friend and colleague <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Weyl" title="Hermann Weyl">Hermann Weyl</a> described her scholarly output in three epochs:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Emmy Noether's scientific production fell into three clearly distinct epochs:
</p><p>(1) the period of relative dependence, 1907–1919
</p><p>(2) the investigations grouped around the general theory of ideals 1920–1926
</p><p>
(3) the study of the non-commutative algebras, their representations by linear transformations, and their application to the study of commutative number fields and their arithmetics</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="#CITEREFWeyl1935">Weyl 1935</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>In the first epoch (1907–1919), Noether dealt primarily with <a href="/wiki/Invariant_theory" title="Invariant theory">differential and algebraic invariants</a>, beginning with her dissertation under <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gordan" title="Paul Gordan">Paul Gordan</a>. Her mathematical horizons broadened, and her work became more general and abstract, as she became acquainted with the work of <a href="/wiki/David_Hilbert" title="David Hilbert">David Hilbert</a>, through close interactions with a successor to Gordan, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Sigismund_Fischer" title="Ernst Sigismund Fischer">Ernst Sigismund Fischer</a>. After moving to Göttingen in 1915, she produced her work for physics, the two <a href="/wiki/Noether%27s_theorem" title="Noether's theorem">Noether's theorems</a>.
</p><p>In the second epoch (1920–1926), Noether devoted herself to developing the theory of <a href="/wiki/Ring_(mathematics)" title="Ring (mathematics)">mathematical rings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilmer1981131_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilmer1981131-86">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the third epoch (1927–1935), Noether focused on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noncommutative_algebra" title="Noncommutative algebra">noncommutative algebra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linear_map" title="Linear map">linear transformations</a>, and commutative number fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198110–23_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198110–23-87">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the results of Noether's first epoch were impressive and useful, her fame among mathematicians rests more on the groundbreaking work she did in her second and third epochs, as noted by Hermann Weyl and B.L. van der Waerden in their obituaries of her.
</p><p>In these epochs, she was not merely applying ideas and methods of earlier mathematicians; rather, she was crafting new systems of mathematical definitions that would be used by future mathematicians. In particular, she developed a completely new theory of <a href="/wiki/Ideal_(ring_theory)" title="Ideal (ring theory)">ideals</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ring_(mathematics)" title="Ring (mathematics)">rings</a>, generalizing earlier work of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dedekind" title="Richard Dedekind">Richard Dedekind</a>. She is also renowned for developing ascending chain conditions, a simple finiteness condition that yielded powerful results in her hands. Such conditions and the theory of ideals enabled Noether to generalize many older results and to treat old problems from a new perspective, such as <a href="/wiki/Elimination_theory" title="Elimination theory">elimination theory</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_variety" title="Algebraic variety">algebraic varieties</a> that had been studied by her father.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_context">Historical context</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Historical context">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the century from 1832 to Noether's death in 1935, the field of mathematics – specifically <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a> – underwent a profound revolution, whose reverberations are still being felt. Mathematicians of previous centuries had worked on practical methods for solving specific types of equations, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Cubic_function" title="Cubic function">cubic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quartic_equation" title="Quartic equation">quartic</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quintic_equation" title="Quintic equation">quintic equations</a>, as well as on the <a href="/wiki/Root_of_unity" title="Root of unity">related problem</a> of constructing <a href="/wiki/Regular_polygon" title="Regular polygon">regular polygons</a> using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compass_and_straightedge_constructions" title="Compass and straightedge constructions">compass and straightedge</a>. Beginning with <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a>'s 1832 proof that <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a> such as five can be <a href="/wiki/Integer_factorization" title="Integer factorization">factored</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_integer" title="Gaussian integer">Gaussian integers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/%C3%89variste_Galois" title="Évariste Galois">Évariste Galois</a>'s introduction of <a href="/wiki/Permutation_group" title="Permutation group">permutation groups</a> in 1832 (although, because of his death, his papers were published only in 1846, by Liouville), <a href="/wiki/William_Rowan_Hamilton" title="William Rowan Hamilton">William Rowan Hamilton</a>'s discovery of <a href="/wiki/Quaternion" title="Quaternion">quaternions</a> in 1843, and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Cayley" title="Arthur Cayley">Arthur Cayley</a>'s more modern definition of groups in 1854, research turned to determining the properties of ever-more-abstract systems defined by ever-more-universal rules. Noether's most important contributions to mathematics were to the development of this new field, <a href="/wiki/Abstract_algebra" title="Abstract algebra">abstract algebra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-algebra_contributions_paramount_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-algebra_contributions_paramount-89">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Background_on_abstract_algebra_and_begriffliche_Mathematik_.28conceptual_mathematics.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Background_on_abstract_algebra_and_begriffliche_Mathematik_(conceptual_mathematics)">Background on abstract algebra and <i>begriffliche Mathematik</i> (conceptual mathematics)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Background on abstract algebra and begriffliche Mathematik (conceptual mathematics)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Two of the most basic objects in abstract algebra are <a href="/wiki/Group_(mathematics)" title="Group (mathematics)">groups</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ring_(mathematics)" title="Ring (mathematics)">rings</a>.
</p><p>A <i>group</i> consists of a set of elements and a single operation which combines a first and a second element and returns a third. The operation must satisfy certain constraints for it to determine a group: It must be <a href="/wiki/Closure_(mathematics)" title="Closure (mathematics)">closed</a> (when applied to any pair of elements of the associated set, the generated element must also be a member of that set), it must be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Associativity" title="Associativity">associative</a>, there must be an <a href="/wiki/Identity_element" title="Identity element">identity element</a> (an element which, when combined with another element using the operation, results in the original element, such as adding zero to a number or multiplying it by one), and for every element there must be an <a href="/wiki/Inverse_element" title="Inverse element">inverse element</a>.
</p><p>A <i>ring</i> likewise, has a set of elements, but now has <i>two</i> operations. The first operation must make the set a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commutativity" title="Commutativity">commutative</a> group, and the second operation is associative and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distributivity" title="Distributivity">distributive</a> with respect to the first operation. It may or may not be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commutativity" title="Commutativity">commutative</a>; this means that the result of applying the operation to a first and a second element is the same as to the second and first – the order of the elements does not matter. If every non-zero element has a <a href="/wiki/Multiplicative_inverse" title="Multiplicative inverse">multiplicative inverse</a> (an element <i>x</i> such that <i>a x</i> = <i>x a</i> = 1 ), the ring is called a <i><a href="/wiki/Division_ring" title="Division ring">division ring</a></i>. A <i><a href="/wiki/Field_(mathematics)" title="Field (mathematics)">field</a></i> is defined as a commutative division ring.
</p><p>Groups are frequently studied through <i><a href="/wiki/Group_representation" title="Group representation">group representations</a></i>. In their most general form, these consist of a choice of group, a set, and an <i>action</i> of the group on the set, that is, an operation which takes an element of the group and an element of the set and returns an element of the set. Most often, the set is a <a href="/wiki/Vector_space" title="Vector space">vector space</a>, and the group represents symmetries of the vector space. For example, there is a group which represents the rigid rotations of space. This is a type of symmetry of space, because space itself does not change when it is rotated even though the positions of objects in it do. Noether used these sorts of symmetries in her work on invariants in physics.
</p><p>A powerful way of studying rings is through their <i><a href="/wiki/Module_(mathematics)" title="Module (mathematics)">modules</a></i>. A module consists of a choice of ring, another set, usually distinct from the underlying set of the ring and called the underlying set of the module, an operation on pairs of elements of the underlying set of the module, and an operation which takes an element of the ring and an element of the module and returns an element of the module.
</p><p>The underlying set of the module and its operation must form a group. A module is a ring-theoretic version of a group representation: Ignoring the second ring operation and the operation on pairs of module elements determines a group representation. The real utility of modules is that the kinds of modules that exist and their interactions, reveal the structure of the ring in ways that are not apparent from the ring itself. An important special case of this is an <i><a href="/wiki/Algebra_over_a_field" title="Algebra over a field">algebra</a></i>. (The word algebra means both a subject within mathematics as well as an object studied in the subject of algebra.) An algebra consists of a choice of two rings and an operation which takes an element from each ring and returns an element of the second ring. This operation makes the second ring into a module over the first. Often the first ring is a field.
</p><p>Words such as "element" and "combining operation" are very general, and can be applied to many real-world and abstract situations. Any set of things that obeys all the rules for one (or two) operation(s) is, by definition, a group (or ring), and obeys all theorems about groups (or rings). Integer numbers, and the operations of addition and multiplication, are just one example. For example, the elements might be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Word_(data_type)" title="Word (data type)">computer data words</a>, where the first combining operation is <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_or" title="Exclusive or">exclusive or</a> and the second is <a href="/wiki/Logical_conjunction" title="Logical conjunction">logical conjunction</a>. Theorems of abstract algebra are powerful because they are general; they govern many systems. It might be imagined that little could be concluded about objects defined with so few properties, but precisely therein lay Noether's gift to discover the maximum that could be concluded from a given set of properties, or conversely, to identify the minimum set, the essential properties responsible for a particular observation. Unlike most mathematicians, she did not make abstractions by generalizing from known examples; rather, she worked directly with the abstractions. In his obituary of Noether, her student van der Waerden recalled that
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The maxim by which Emmy Noether was guided throughout her work might be formulated as follows: "<i>Any relationships between numbers, functions, and operations become transparent, generally applicable, and fully productive only after they have been isolated from their particular objects and been formulated as universally valid concepts.</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick1981101_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick1981101-90">[88]</a></sup> </p></blockquote>
<p>This is the <i>begriffliche Mathematik</i> (purely conceptual mathematics) that was characteristic of Noether. This style of mathematics was consequently adopted by other mathematicians, especially in the (then new) field of abstract algebra.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Example:_Integers_as_a_ring">Example: Integers as a ring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Example: Integers as a ring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integers</a> form a commutative ring whose elements are the integers, and the combining operations are addition and multiplication. Any pair of integers can be <a href="/wiki/Addition" title="Addition">added</a> or <a href="/wiki/Multiplication" title="Multiplication">multiplied</a>, always resulting in another integer, and the first operation, addition, is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commutativity" title="Commutativity">commutative</a>, i.e., for any elements <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> in the ring, <i>a</i> + <i>b</i> = <i>b</i> + <i>a</i>. The second operation, multiplication, also is commutative, but that need not be true for other rings, meaning that <i>a</i> combined with <i>b</i> might be different from <i>b</i> combined with <i>a</i>. Examples of noncommutative rings include <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(mathematics)" title="Matrix (mathematics)">matrices</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quaternion" title="Quaternion">quaternions</a>. The integers do not form a division ring, because the second operation cannot always be inverted; there is no integer <i>a</i> such that 3 × <i>a</i> = 1.
</p><p>The integers have additional properties which do not generalize to all commutative rings. An important example is the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_arithmetic" title="Fundamental theorem of arithmetic">fundamental theorem of arithmetic</a>, which says that every positive integer can be factored uniquely into <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a>. Unique factorizations do not always exist in other rings, but Noether found a unique factorization theorem, now called the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lasker%E2%80%93Noether_theorem" title="Lasker–Noether theorem">Lasker–Noether theorem</a></i>, for the <a href="/wiki/Ideal_(ring_theory)" title="Ideal (ring theory)">ideals</a> of many rings. Much of Noether's work lay in determining what properties <i>do</i> hold for all rings, in devising novel analogs of the old integer theorems, and in determining the minimal set of assumptions required to yield certain properties of rings.
</p>
<h3><span id="First_epoch_.281908.E2.80.931919.29:_Algebraic_invariant_theory"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_epoch_(1908–1919):_Algebraic_invariant_theory">First epoch (1908–1919): Algebraic invariant theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: First epoch (1908–1919): Algebraic invariant theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emmy_Noether_-_Table_of_invariants_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2676" data-file-width="3533" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Emmy_Noether_-_Table_of_invariants_2.jpg/250px-Emmy_Noether_-_Table_of_invariants_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Emmy_Noether_-_Table_of_invariants_2.jpg/375px-Emmy_Noether_-_Table_of_invariants_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Emmy_Noether_-_Table_of_invariants_2.jpg/500px-Emmy_Noether_-_Table_of_invariants_2.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emmy_Noether_-_Table_of_invariants_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Table 2 from Noether's dissertation <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoether1908_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoether1908-91">[89]</a></sup> on invariant theory. This table collects 202 of the 331 invariants of ternary biquadratic forms. These forms are graded in two variables <i>x</i> and <i>u</i>. The horizontal direction of the table lists the invariants with increasing grades in <i>x</i>, while the vertical direction lists them with increasing grades in <i>u</i>.</div></div></div>
<p>Much of Noether's work in the first epoch of her career was associated with <a href="/wiki/Invariant_theory" title="Invariant theory">invariant theory</a>, principally <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Algebraic_invariant_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Algebraic invariant theory (page does not exist)">algebraic invariant theory</a>. Invariant theory is concerned with expressions that remain constant (invariant) under a <a href="/wiki/Group_(mathematics)" title="Group (mathematics)">group</a> of transformations. As an everyday example, if a rigid yardstick is rotated, the coordinates (<i>x</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>y</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>z</i><sub>1</sub>) and (<i>x</i><sub>2</sub>, <i>y</i><sub>2</sub>, <i>z</i><sub>2</sub>) of its endpoints change, but its length <i>L</i> given by the formula  <i>L</i><sup>2</sup> = Δ<i>x</i><sup>2</sup> + Δ<i>y</i><sup>2</sup> + Δ<i>z</i><sup>2</sup> remains the same. Invariant theory was an active area of research in the later nineteenth century, prompted in part by <a href="/wiki/Felix_Klein" title="Felix Klein">Felix Klein</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Erlangen_program" title="Erlangen program">Erlangen program</a>, according to which different types of <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a> should be characterized by their invariants under transformations, e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Cross-ratio" title="Cross-ratio">cross-ratio</a> of <a href="/wiki/Projective_geometry" title="Projective geometry">projective geometry</a>.
</p><p>An example of an <i>invariant</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Discriminant" title="Discriminant">discriminant</a> <i>B</i><sup>2</sup> − 4 <i>A C</i> of a binary <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_form" title="Quadratic form">quadratic form</a> <b>x·</b>A <b>x</b> + <b>y·</b>B <b>x</b> + <b>y·</b>C <b>y</b> , where <b>x</b> and <b>y</b> are <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_vector" title="Euclidean vector">vectors</a> and "<b>·</b>" is the <a href="/wiki/Dot_product" title="Dot product">dot product</a> or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_product" title="Inner product">inner product</a>" for the vectors. A, B, and C are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_operator" title="Linear operator">linear operators</a> on the vectors – typically <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(mathematics)" title="Matrix (mathematics)">matrices</a>.
</p><p>The discriminant is called "invariant" because it is not changed by linear substitutions <i><b>x</b></i> → <i>a</i> <i><b>x</b></i> + <i>b</i> <i><b>y</b></i>, <i>y</i> → <i>c</i> <b>x</b> + <i>d</i> <b>y</b> with determinant <i>a</i> <i>d</i> − <i>b</i> <i>c</i> = 1 . These substitutions form the <a href="/wiki/Special_linear_group" title="Special linear group">special linear group</a> <i>SL</i><sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[c]</a></sup>
</p><p>One can ask for all polynomials in A, B, and C that are unchanged by the action of <i>SL</i><sub>2</sub>; these are called the invariants of binary quadratic forms and turn out to be the polynomials in the discriminant.
</p><p>More generally, one can ask for the invariants of homogeneous polynomials A<sub>0</sub> <i>x</i><sup>r</sup> <i>y</i><sup>0</sup> + ... + A<sub>r</sub> x<sup>0</sup> <i>y</i><sup>r</sup> of higher degree, which will be certain polynomials in the coefficients A<sub>0</sub>, ..., A<sub>r</sub>, and more generally still, one can ask the similar question for homogeneous polynomials in more than two variables.
</p><p>One of the main goals of invariant theory was to solve the "<i>finite basis problem</i>". The sum or product of any two invariants is invariant, and the finite basis problem asked whether it was possible to get all the invariants by starting with a finite list of invariants, called <i>generators</i>, and then, adding or multiplying the generators together. For example, the discriminant gives a finite basis (with one element) for the invariants of binary quadratic forms.
</p><p>Noether's advisor, Paul Gordan, was known as the "king of invariant theory", and his chief contribution to mathematics was his 1870 solution of the finite basis problem for invariants of homogeneous polynomials in two variables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoether191411_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoether191411-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGordan1870_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGordan1870-94">[91]</a></sup> He proved this by giving a constructive method for finding all of the invariants and their generators, but was not able to carry out this constructive approach for invariants in three or more variables. In 1890, David Hilbert proved a similar statement for the invariants of homogeneous polynomials in any number of variables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeyl1944618–21_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeyl1944618–21-95">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHilbert1890531_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHilbert1890531-96">[93]</a></sup> Furthermore, his method worked, not only for the special linear group, but also for some of its subgroups such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_orthogonal_group" title="Special orthogonal group">special orthogonal group</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHilbert1890532_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHilbert1890532-97">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="First_epoch_.281908.E2.80.931919.29:_Galois_theory"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_epoch_(1908–1919):_Galois_theory">First epoch (1908–1919): Galois theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: First epoch (1908–1919): Galois theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Galois_theory" title="Galois theory">Galois theory</a> concerns transformations of <a href="/wiki/Field_(mathematics)" title="Field (mathematics)">number fields</a> that <a href="/wiki/Permutation" title="Permutation">permute</a> the roots of an equation. Consider a polynomial equation of a variable <i>x</i> of <a href="/wiki/Degree_of_a_polynomial" title="Degree of a polynomial">degree</a> <i>n</i>, in which the coefficients are drawn from some <a href="/wiki/Ground_field" title="Ground field">ground field</a>, which might be, for example, the field of <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real numbers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rational_number" title="Rational number">rational numbers</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integers</a> <a href="/wiki/Modular_arithmetic" title="Modular arithmetic">modulo</a> 7. There may or may not be choices of <i>x</i>, which make this polynomial evaluate to zero. Such choices, if they exist, are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Root_of_a_function" title="Root of a function">roots</a>. If the polynomial is <i>x</i><sup>2</sup> + 1 and the field is the real numbers, then the polynomial has no roots, because any choice of <i>x</i> makes the polynomial greater than or equal to one. If the field is <a href="/wiki/Field_extension" title="Field extension">extended</a>, however, then the polynomial may gain roots, and if it is extended enough, then it always has a number of roots equal to its degree.
</p><p>Continuing the previous example, if the field is enlarged to the complex numbers, then the polynomial gains two roots, +<i>i</i> and −<i>i</i>, where <i>i</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Imaginary_unit" title="Imaginary unit">imaginary unit</a>, that is, <i>i</i><sup> 2</sup> = −1 . More generally, the extension field in which a polynomial can be factored into its roots is known as the <a href="/wiki/Splitting_field" title="Splitting field">splitting field</a> of the polynomial.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Galois_group" title="Galois group">Galois group</a> of a polynomial is the set of all transformations of the splitting field which preserve the ground field and the roots of the polynomial. (In mathematical jargon, these transformations are called <a href="/wiki/Automorphism" title="Automorphism">automorphisms</a>.) The Galois group of <span class="nowrap"><i>x</i><sup>2</sup> + 1</span> consists of two elements: The identity transformation, which sends every complex number to itself, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_conjugation" title="Complex conjugation">complex conjugation</a>, which sends +<i>i</i> to −<i>i</i>. Since the Galois group does not change the ground field, it leaves the coefficients of the polynomial unchanged, so it must leave the set of all roots unchanged. Each root can move to another root, however, so transformation determines a <a href="/wiki/Permutation" title="Permutation">permutation</a> of the <i>n</i> roots among themselves. The significance of the Galois group derives from the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_Galois_theory" title="Fundamental theorem of Galois theory">fundamental theorem of Galois theory</a>, which proves that the fields lying between the ground field and the splitting field are in one-to-one correspondence with the <a href="/wiki/Subgroup" title="Subgroup">subgroups</a> of the Galois group.
</p><p>In 1918, Noether published a paper on the <a href="/wiki/Inverse_Galois_problem" title="Inverse Galois problem">inverse Galois problem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup> Instead of determining the Galois group of transformations of a given field and its extension, Noether asked whether, given a field and a group, it always is possible to find an extension of the field that has the given group as its Galois group. She reduced this to "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noether%27s_problem" title="Noether's problem">Noether's problem</a>", which asks whether the fixed field of a subgroup <i>G</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Symmetric_group" title="Symmetric group">permutation group</a> <i>S</i><sub><i>n</i></sub> acting on the field  <i>k</i>(<i>x</i><sub>1</sub>, ... , <i>x</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>) always is a pure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcendental_extension" title="Transcendental extension">transcendental extension</a> of the field <i>k</i>. (She first mentioned this problem in a 1913 paper,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup> where she attributed the problem to her colleague <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Sigismund_Fischer" title="Ernst Sigismund Fischer">Fischer</a>.) She showed this was true for  <i>n</i> = 2, 3, or 4. In 1969, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=R.G._Swan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="R.G. Swan (page does not exist)">R.G. Swan</a> found a counter-example to Noether's problem, with  <i>n</i> = 47 and <i>G</i> a <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_group" title="Cyclic group">cyclic group</a> of order 47<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup> (although this group can be realized as a <a href="/wiki/Galois_group" title="Galois group">Galois group</a> over the rationals in other ways). The inverse Galois problem remains unsolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="First_epoch_.281908.E2.80.931919.29:_Physics"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_epoch_(1908–1919):_Physics">First epoch (1908–1919): Physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: First epoch (1908–1919): Physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Noether%27s_theorem" title="Noether's theorem">Noether's theorem</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_law_(physics)" title="Conservation law (physics)">Conservation law (physics)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Constant_of_motion" title="Constant of motion">Constant of motion</a></div><p>
Noether was brought to <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen" title="Göttingen">Göttingen</a> in 1915 by David Hilbert and Felix Klein, who wanted her expertise in invariant theory to help them in understanding <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>, a geometrical theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a> developed mainly by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>. Hilbert had observed that the <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_energy" title="Conservation of energy">conservation of energy</a> seemed to be violated in general relativity, because gravitational energy could itself gravitate. Noether provided the resolution of this paradox, and a fundamental tool of modern <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical physics</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Noether%27s_theorem" title="Noether's theorem">Noether's first theorem</a>, which she proved in 1915, but did not publish until 1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup> She not only solved the problem for general relativity, but also determined the conserved quantities for <i>every</i> system of physical laws that possesses some continuous symmetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup> Upon receiving her work, Einstein wrote to Hilbert:<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Yesterday I received from Miss Noether a very interesting paper on invariants. I'm impressed that such things can be understood in such a general way. The old guard at Göttingen should take some lessons from Miss Noether! She seems to know her stuff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>For illustration, if a physical system behaves the same, regardless of how it is oriented in space, the physical laws that govern it are rotationally symmetric; from this symmetry, Noether's theorem shows the <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a> of the system must be conserved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ledhill_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-ledhill-105">[102]</a></sup> The physical system itself need not be symmetric; a jagged asteroid tumbling in space <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum" title="Conservation of angular momentum">conserves angular momentum</a> despite its asymmetry. Rather, the symmetry of the <i>physical laws</i> governing the system is responsible for the conservation law. As another example, if a physical experiment has the same outcome at any place and at any time, then its laws are symmetric under continuous translations in space and time; by Noether's theorem, these symmetries account for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_law_(physics)" title="Conservation law (physics)">conservation laws</a> of <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">linear momentum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> within this system, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Noether's theorem has become a fundamental tool of modern <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical physics</a>, both because of the insight it gives into conservation laws, and also, as a practical calculation tool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neeman_1999_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-neeman_1999-5">[4]</a></sup> Her theorem allows researchers to determine the conserved quantities from the observed symmetries of a physical system. Conversely, it facilitates the description of a physical system based on classes of hypothetical physical laws. For illustration, suppose that a new physical phenomenon is discovered. Noether's theorem provides a test for theoretical models of the phenomenon:</p><blockquote><p>If the theory has a continuous symmetry, then Noether's theorem guarantees that the theory has a conserved quantity, and for the theory to be correct, this conservation must be observable in experiments.</p></blockquote>
<h3><span id="Second_epoch_.281920.E2.80.931926.29:_Ascending_and_descending_chain_conditions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_epoch_(1920–1926):_Ascending_and_descending_chain_conditions">Second epoch (1920–1926): Ascending and descending chain conditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Second epoch (1920–1926): Ascending and descending chain conditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In this epoch, Noether became famous for her deft use of ascending (<i>Teilerkettensatz</i>) or descending (<i>Vielfachenkettensatz</i>) chain conditions. A sequence of <a href="/wiki/Empty_set" title="Empty set">non-empty</a> <a href="/wiki/Subset" title="Subset">subsets</a> <i>A</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>A</i><sub>2</sub>, <i>A</i><sub>3</sub>, etc. of a <a href="/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set (mathematics)">set</a> <i>S</i> is usually said to be <i>ascending</i>, if each is a subset of the next
</p>

<p>Conversely, a sequence of subsets of <i>S</i> is called <i>descending</i> if each contains the next subset:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle A_{1}\supset A_{2}\supset A_{3}\supset \cdots .}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</msub>
<mo>⊃<!-- ⊃ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>A</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>

</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="A_{1} \supset A_{2} \supset A_{3} \supset \cdots." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/df973947674889d97da16e383b471d0a237e91ab" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:21.445ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>A chain <i>becomes constant after a finite number of steps</i> if there is an <i>n</i> such that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle A_{n}=A_{m}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>A</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>A</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle A_{n}=A_{m}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle A_{n}=A_{m}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/68697c0554c419ec4acfc9de7418abdfe4f5f9ef" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:9.478ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> for all <i>m</i> ≥ <i>n</i>. A collection of subsets of a given set satisfies the <a href="/wiki/Ascending_chain_condition" title="Ascending chain condition">ascending chain condition</a> if any ascending sequence becomes constant after a finite number of steps. It satisfies the descending chain condition if any descending sequence becomes constant after a finite number of steps.
</p><p>Ascending and descending chain conditions are general, meaning that they can be applied to many types of mathematical objects—and, on the surface, they might not seem very powerful. Noether showed how to exploit such conditions, however, to maximum advantage.
</p><p>For example: How to use chain conditions to show that every set of sub-objects has a maximal/minimal element or that a complex object can be generated by a smaller number of elements. These conclusions often are crucial steps in a proof.
</p><p>Many types of objects in <a href="/wiki/Abstract_algebra" title="Abstract algebra">abstract algebra</a> can satisfy chain conditions, and usually if they satisfy an ascending chain condition, they are called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noetherian_(disambiguation)" title="Noetherian (disambiguation)">Noetherian</a></i> in her honor. By definition, a <a href="/wiki/Noetherian_ring" title="Noetherian ring">Noetherian ring</a> satisfies an ascending chain condition on its left and right ideals, whereas a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noetherian_group" title="Noetherian group">Noetherian group</a> is defined as a group in which every strictly ascending chain of subgroups is finite. A <a href="/wiki/Noetherian_module" title="Noetherian module">Noetherian module</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Module_(mathematics)" title="Module (mathematics)">module</a> in which every strictly ascending chain of submodules becomes constant after a finite number of steps. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noetherian_space" title="Noetherian space">Noetherian space</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Topological_space" title="Topological space">topological space</a> in which every strictly ascending chain of open subspaces becomes constant after a finite number of steps; this definition makes the <a href="/wiki/Spectrum_of_a_ring" title="Spectrum of a ring">spectrum</a> of a Noetherian ring a Noetherian topological space.
</p><p>The chain condition often is "inherited" by sub-objects. For example, all subspaces of a Noetherian space, are Noetherian themselves; all subgroups and quotient groups of a Noetherian group are likewise, Noetherian; and, <i><a href="/wiki/Mutatis_mutandis" title="Mutatis mutandis">mutatis mutandis</a></i>, the same holds for submodules and quotient modules of a Noetherian module. All quotient rings of a Noetherian ring are Noetherian, but that does not necessarily hold for its subrings. The chain condition also may be inherited by combinations or extensions of a Noetherian object. For example, finite direct sums of Noetherian rings are Noetherian, as is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ring_of_formal_power_series" title="Ring of formal power series">ring of formal power series</a> over a Noetherian ring.
</p><p>Another application of such chain conditions is in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noetherian_induction" title="Noetherian induction">Noetherian induction</a>—also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Well-founded_induction" title="Well-founded induction">well-founded induction</a>—which is a generalization of <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_induction" title="Mathematical induction">mathematical induction</a>. It frequently is used to reduce general statements about collections of objects to statements about specific objects in that collection. Suppose that <i>S</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Partially_ordered_set" title="Partially ordered set">partially ordered set</a>. One way of proving a statement about the objects of <i>S</i> is to assume the existence of a <a href="/wiki/Counterexample" title="Counterexample">counterexample</a> and deduce a contradiction, thereby proving the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contrapositive" title="Contrapositive">contrapositive</a> of the original statement. The basic premise of Noetherian induction is that every non-empty subset of <i>S</i> contains a minimal element. In particular, the set of all counterexamples contains a minimal element, the <i>minimal counterexample</i>. In order to prove the original statement, therefore, it suffices to prove something seemingly much weaker: For any counter-example, there is a smaller counter-example.
</p>
<h3><span id="Second_epoch_.281920.E2.80.931926.29:_Commutative_rings.2C_ideals.2C_and_modules"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_epoch_(1920–1926):_Commutative_rings,_ideals,_and_modules">Second epoch (1920–1926): Commutative rings, ideals, and modules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Second epoch (1920–1926): Commutative rings, ideals, and modules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Noether's paper, <i>Idealtheorie in Ringbereichen</i> (<i>Theory of Ideals in Ring Domains</i>, 1921),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoether1921_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoether1921-107">[104]</a></sup> is the foundation of general commutative <a href="/wiki/Ring_theory" title="Ring theory">ring theory</a>, and gives one of the first general definitions of a <a href="/wiki/Commutative_ring" title="Commutative ring">commutative ring</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gil133_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gil133-108">[105]</a></sup> Before her paper, most results in commutative algebra were restricted to special examples of commutative rings, such as polynomial rings over fields or rings of algebraic integers. Noether proved that in a ring which satisfies the ascending chain condition on <a href="/wiki/Ideal_(ring_theory)" title="Ideal (ring theory)">ideals</a>, every ideal is finitely generated. In 1943, French mathematician <a href="/wiki/Claude_Chevalley" title="Claude Chevalley">Claude Chevalley</a> coined the term, <i><a href="/wiki/Noetherian_ring" title="Noetherian ring">Noetherian ring</a></i>, to describe this property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gil133_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gil133-108">[105]</a></sup> A major result in Noether's 1921 paper is the <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lasker%E2%80%93Noether_theorem" title="Lasker–Noether theorem">Lasker–Noether theorem</a></b>, which extends Lasker's theorem on the primary decomposition of ideals of polynomial rings to all Noetherian rings. The Lasker–Noether theorem can be viewed as a generalization of the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_arithmetic" title="Fundamental theorem of arithmetic">fundamental theorem of arithmetic</a> which states that any positive integer can be expressed as a product of <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a>, and that this decomposition is unique.
</p><p>Noether's work <i>Abstrakter Aufbau der Idealtheorie in algebraischen Zahl- und Funktionenkörpern</i> (<i>Abstract Structure of the Theory of Ideals in Algebraic Number and Function Fields</i>, 1927)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup> characterized the rings in which the ideals have unique factorization into prime ideals as the <a href="/wiki/Dedekind_domain" title="Dedekind domain">Dedekind domains</a>: integral domains that are Noetherian, 0- or 1-<a href="/wiki/Krull_dimension" title="Krull dimension">dimensional</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integrality" title="Integrality">integrally closed</a> in their quotient fields. This paper also contains what now are called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isomorphism_theorem" title="Isomorphism theorem">isomorphism theorems</a>, which describe some fundamental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_isomorphism" title="Natural isomorphism">natural isomorphisms</a>, and some other basic results on Noetherian and <a href="/wiki/Artinian_module" title="Artinian module">Artinian modules</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Second_epoch_.281920.E2.80.931926.29:_Elimination_theory"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_epoch_(1920–1926):_Elimination_theory">Second epoch (1920–1926): Elimination theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Second epoch (1920–1926): Elimination theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1923–1924, Noether applied her ideal theory to <a href="/wiki/Elimination_theory" title="Elimination theory">elimination theory</a> in a formulation that she attributed to her student, Kurt Hentzelt. She showed that fundamental theorems about the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polynomial_factorization" title="Polynomial factorization">factorization of polynomials</a> could be carried over directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoether1923_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoether1923-110">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoether1923b_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoether1923b-111">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoether1924_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoether1924-112">[109]</a></sup> Traditionally, elimination theory is concerned with eliminating one or more variables from a system of polynomial equations, usually by the method of <a href="/wiki/Resultant" title="Resultant">resultants</a>.
</p><p>For illustration, a system of equations often can be written in the form   M <b>v</b> = <b>0</b>   where a matrix (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_transform" title="Linear transform">linear transform</a>)   M   (without the variable <i>x</i>) times a vector <b>v</b> (that only has non-zero powers of <i>x</i>) is equal to the zero vector, <b>0</b>. Hence, the <a href="/wiki/Determinant" title="Determinant">determinant</a> of the matrix   M   must be zero, providing a new equation in which the variable <i>x</i> has been eliminated.
</p>
<h3><span id="Second_epoch_.281920.E2.80.931926.29:_Invariant_theory_of_finite_groups"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_epoch_(1920–1926):_Invariant_theory_of_finite_groups">Second epoch (1920–1926): Invariant theory of finite groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Second epoch (1920–1926): Invariant theory of finite groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Techniques such as Hilbert's original non-constructive solution to the finite basis problem could not be used to get quantitative information about the invariants of a group action, and furthermore, they did not apply to all group actions. In her 1915 paper,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoether1915_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoether1915-113">[110]</a></sup> Noether found a solution to the finite basis problem for a finite group of transformations   <i>G</i>   acting on a finite-dimensional vector space over a field of characteristic zero. Her solution shows that the ring of invariants is generated by homogeneous invariants whose degree is less than, or equal to, the order of the finite group; this is called <b>Noether's bound</b>. Her paper gave two proofs of Noether's bound, both of which also work when the characteristic of the field is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coprime" title="Coprime">coprime</a> to |<i>G</i>|! (the <a href="/wiki/Factorial" title="Factorial">factorial</a> of the order |<i>G</i>| of the group <i>G</i>). The degrees of generators need not satisfy Noether's bound when the characteristic of the field divides the number |<i>G</i>|,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFleischmann200024_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFleischmann200024-114">[111]</a></sup> but Noether was not able to determine whether this bound was correct when the characteristic of the field divides |<i>G</i>|! but not |<i>G</i>|. For many years, determining the truth or falsehood of this bound for this particular case was an open problem, called "Noether's gap". It was finally solved independently by Fleischmann in 2000 and Fogarty in 2001, who both showed that the bound remains true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFleischmann200025_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFleischmann200025-115">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFogarty20015_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFogarty20015-116">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>In her 1926 paper,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoether1926_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoether1926-117">[114]</a></sup> Noether extended Hilbert's theorem to representations of a finite group over any field; the new case that did not follow from Hilbert's work is when the characteristic of the field divides the order of the group. Noether's result was later extended by <a href="/wiki/William_Haboush" title="William Haboush">William Haboush</a> to all reductive groups by his proof of the <a href="/wiki/Haboush%27s_theorem" title="Haboush's theorem">Mumford conjecture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaboush1975_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaboush1975-118">[115]</a></sup> In this paper Noether also introduced the <i><a href="/wiki/Noether_normalization_lemma" title="Noether normalization lemma">Noether normalization lemma</a></i>, showing that a finitely generated <a href="/wiki/Integral_domain" title="Integral domain">domain</a> <i>A</i> over a field <i>k</i> has a set {<i>x</i><sub>1</sub>, ..., <i>x</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>} of <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_independence" title="Algebraic independence">algebraically independent</a> elements such that <i>A</i> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integrality" title="Integrality">integral</a> over <i>k</i>[<i>x</i><sub>1</sub>, ..., <i>x</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>].
</p>
<h3><span id="Second_epoch_.281920.E2.80.931926.29:_Contributions_to_topology"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_epoch_(1920–1926):_Contributions_to_topology">Second epoch (1920–1926): Contributions to topology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Second epoch (1920–1926): Contributions to topology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mug_and_Torus_morph.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="240" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/26/Mug_and_Torus_morph.gif" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mug_and_Torus_morph.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A continuous deformation (<a href="/wiki/Homotopy" title="Homotopy">homotopy</a>) of a coffee cup into a doughnut (<a href="/wiki/Torus" title="Torus">torus</a>) and back</div></div></div>
<p>As noted by <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Alexandrov" title="Pavel Alexandrov">Pavel Alexandrov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Weyl" title="Hermann Weyl">Hermann Weyl</a> in their obituaries, Noether's contributions to <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topology</a> illustrate her generosity with ideas and how her insights could transform entire fields of mathematics. In topology, mathematicians study the properties of objects that remain invariant even under deformation, properties such as their <a href="/wiki/Connected_space" title="Connected space">connectedness</a>. An old joke is that "<i>a topologist cannot distinguish a donut from a coffee mug</i>", since they can be continuously deformed into one another.
</p><p>Noether is credited with fundamental ideas that led to the development of <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_topology" title="Algebraic topology">algebraic topology</a> from the earlier <a href="/wiki/Combinatorial_topology" title="Combinatorial topology">combinatorial topology</a>, specifically, the idea of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homology_theory#Towards_algebraic_topology" title="Homology theory">homology groups</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHilton1988284_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHilton1988284-119">[116]</a></sup> According to the account of Alexandrov, Noether attended lectures given by <a href="/wiki/Heinz_Hopf" title="Heinz Hopf">Heinz Hopf</a> and by him in the summers of 1926 and 1927, where "she continually made observations which were often deep and subtle"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick1981173_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick1981173-120">[117]</a></sup> and he continues that,
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When ... she first became acquainted with a systematic construction of combinatorial topology, she immediately observed that it would be worthwhile to study directly the <a href="/wiki/Group_(mathematics)" title="Group (mathematics)">groups</a> of algebraic complexes and cycles of a given polyhedron and the <a href="/wiki/Subgroup" title="Subgroup">subgroup</a> of the cycle group consisting of cycles homologous to zero; instead of the usual definition of <a href="/wiki/Betti_number" title="Betti number">Betti numbers</a>, she suggested immediately defining the Betti group as the <a href="/wiki/Quotient_group" title="Quotient group">complementary (quotient) group</a> of the group of all cycles by the subgroup of cycles homologous to zero. This observation now seems self-evident. But in those years (1925–1928) this was a completely new point of view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick1981174_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick1981174-121">[118]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Noether's suggestion that topology be studied algebraically was adopted immediately by Hopf, Alexandrov, and others,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick1981174_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick1981174-121">[118]</a></sup> and it became a frequent topic of discussion among the mathematicians of Göttingen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirzebruch_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hirzebruch-122">[119]</a></sup> Noether observed that her idea of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Betti_group" title="Betti group">Betti group</a> makes the <a href="/wiki/Euler_characteristic" title="Euler characteristic">Euler–Poincaré formula</a> simpler to understand, and Hopf's own work on this subject<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopf1928_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopf1928-123">[120]</a></sup> "bears the imprint of these remarks of Emmy Noether".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick1981174–75_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick1981174–75-124">[121]</a></sup> Noether mentions her own topology ideas only as an aside in a 1926 publication,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoether1926b_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoether1926b-125">[122]</a></sup> where she cites it as an application of <a href="/wiki/Group_theory" title="Group theory">group theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>This algebraic approach to topology was also developed independently in <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>. In a 1926–1927 course given in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Vietoris" title="Leopold Vietoris">Leopold Vietoris</a> defined a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homology_group" title="Homology group">homology group</a>, which was developed by <a href="/wiki/Walther_Mayer" title="Walther Mayer">Walther Mayer</a>, into an axiomatic definition in 1928.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Helmut_Hasse.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="257" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Helmut_Hasse.jpg/170px-Helmut_Hasse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Helmut_Hasse.jpg/255px-Helmut_Hasse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dd/Helmut_Hasse.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Helmut_Hasse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Helmut_Hasse" title="Helmut Hasse">Helmut Hasse</a> worked with Noether and others to found the theory of <a href="/wiki/Central_simple_algebra" title="Central simple algebra">central simple algebras</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Third_epoch_.281927.E2.80.931935.29:_Hypercomplex_numbers_and_representation_theory"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_epoch_(1927–1935):_Hypercomplex_numbers_and_representation_theory">Third epoch (1927–1935): Hypercomplex numbers and representation theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Third epoch (1927–1935): Hypercomplex numbers and representation theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Much work on <a href="/wiki/Hypercomplex_number" title="Hypercomplex number">hypercomplex numbers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Group_representation" title="Group representation">group representations</a> was carried out in the nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, but remained disparate. Noether united these results and gave the first general representation theory of groups and algebras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noether_1929_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-noether_1929-128">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Briefly, Noether subsumed the structure theory of <a href="/wiki/Associative_algebra" title="Associative algebra">associative algebras</a> and the representation theory of groups into a single arithmetic theory of <a href="/wiki/Module_(mathematics)" title="Module (mathematics)">modules</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ideal_(ring_theory)" title="Ideal (ring theory)">ideals</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ring_(mathematics)" title="Ring (mathematics)">rings</a> satisfying <a href="/wiki/Ascending_chain_condition" title="Ascending chain condition">ascending chain conditions</a>. This single work by Noether was of fundamental importance for the development of modern algebra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden1985244_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Waerden1985244-129">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Third_epoch_.281927.E2.80.931935.29:_Noncommutative_algebra"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_epoch_(1927–1935):_Noncommutative_algebra">Third epoch (1927–1935): Noncommutative algebra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Third epoch (1927–1935): Noncommutative algebra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Noether also was responsible for a number of other advances in the field of algebra. With <a href="/wiki/Emil_Artin" title="Emil Artin">Emil Artin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Brauer" title="Richard Brauer">Richard Brauer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Helmut_Hasse" title="Helmut Hasse">Helmut Hasse</a>, she founded the theory of <a href="/wiki/Central_simple_algebra" title="Central simple algebra">central simple algebras</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELam1981152–53_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELam1981152–53-130">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>
A paper by Noether, Helmut Hasse, and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Brauer" title="Richard Brauer">Richard Brauer</a> pertains to <a href="/wiki/Division_algebra" title="Division algebra">division algebras</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hasse_1932_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-hasse_1932-131">[128]</a></sup> which are algebraic systems in which division is possible. They proved two important theorems: a <a href="/wiki/Hasse_principle" title="Hasse principle">local-global theorem</a> stating that if a finite-dimensional central division algebra over a <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_number_field" title="Algebraic number field">number field</a> splits locally everywhere then it splits globally (so is trivial), and from this, deduced their <i>Hauptsatz</i> ("main theorem"):</p><blockquote><p>every finite dimensional <a href="/wiki/Central_simple_algebra" title="Central simple algebra">central</a> <a href="/wiki/Division_algebra" title="Division algebra">division algebra</a> over an <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_number" title="Algebraic number">algebraic number</a> <a href="/wiki/Field_(mathematics)" title="Field (mathematics)">field</a> F splits over a <a href="/wiki/Abelian_extension" title="Abelian extension">cyclic cyclotomic extension</a>.</p></blockquote><p>These theorems allow one to classify all finite-dimensional central division algebras over a given number field. A subsequent paper by Noether showed, as a special case of a more general theorem, that all maximal subfields of a division algebra <i>D</i> are <a href="/wiki/Central_simple_algebra#Splitting_field" title="Central simple algebra">splitting fields</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoether1933_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoether1933-132">[129]</a></sup> This paper also contains the <a href="/wiki/Skolem%E2%80%93Noether_theorem" title="Skolem–Noether theorem">Skolem–Noether theorem</a> which states that any two embeddings of an extension of a field <i>k</i> into a finite-dimensional central simple algebra over <i>k</i>, are conjugate. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brauer%E2%80%93Noether_theorem" title="Brauer–Noether theorem">Brauer–Noether theorem</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrauerNoether1927_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrauerNoether1927-133">[130]</a></sup> gives a characterization of the splitting fields of a central division algebra over a field.
</p><h2><span id="Assessment.2C_recognition.2C_and_memorials"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Assessment,_recognition,_and_memorials">Assessment, recognition, and memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Assessment, recognition, and memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Emmy_Noether#Other" title="List of things named after Emmy Noether">List of things named after Emmy Noether § Other</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emmy-noether-campus_siegen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="978" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Emmy-noether-campus_siegen.jpg/250px-Emmy-noether-campus_siegen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Emmy-noether-campus_siegen.jpg/375px-Emmy-noether-campus_siegen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Emmy-noether-campus_siegen.jpg/500px-Emmy-noether-campus_siegen.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emmy-noether-campus_siegen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Emmy Noether Campus at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Siegen" title="University of Siegen">University of Siegen</a> is home to its mathematics and physics departments.</div></div></div>
<p>Noether's work continues to be relevant for the development of theoretical physics and mathematics and she is consistently ranked as one of the greatest mathematicians of the twentieth century. In his obituary, fellow algebraist <a href="/wiki/Bartel_Leendert_van_der_Waerden" title="Bartel Leendert van der Waerden">BL van der Waerden</a> says that her mathematical originality was "absolute beyond comparison",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick1981100_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick1981100-134">[131]</a></sup> and Hermann Weyl said that Noether "changed the face of <a href="/wiki/Abstract_algebra" title="Abstract algebra">algebra</a> by her work".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weyl_128_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-weyl_128-9">[8]</a></sup> During her lifetime and even until today, Noether has been characterized as the greatest woman mathematician in recorded history by mathematicians<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexandrov1981100_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexandrov1981100-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames2002321_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames2002321-135">[132]</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Alexandrov" title="Pavel Alexandrov">Pavel Alexandrov</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick1981154_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick1981154-136">[133]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Weyl" title="Hermann Weyl">Hermann Weyl</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDick1981152_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDick1981152-137">[134]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Dieudonn%C3%A9" title="Jean Dieudonné">Jean Dieudonné</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g_noether_p167_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-g_noether_p167-138">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a letter to <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> wrote:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-einstein_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-einstein-3">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In the judgment of the most competent living mathematicians, Fräulein Noether was the most significant creative mathematical <a href="/wiki/Genius" title="Genius">genius</a> thus far produced since the higher education of women began. In the realm of algebra, in which the most gifted mathematicians have been busy for centuries, she discovered methods which have proved of enormous importance in the development of the present-day younger generation of mathematicians.</p></blockquote>
<p>On 2 January 1935, a few months before her death, mathematician <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Wiener" title="Norbert Wiener">Norbert Wiener</a> wrote that <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimberling198135_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimberling198135-139">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Miss Noether is ... the greatest woman mathematician who has ever lived; and the greatest woman scientist of any sort now living, and a scholar at least on the plane of <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Madame Curie</a>.</p></blockquote>
<p>At an exhibition at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1964_World%27s_Fair" title="1964 World's Fair">1964 World's Fair</a> devoted to <a href="/wiki/Mathematica:_A_World_of_Numbers..._and_Beyond" title="Mathematica: A World of Numbers... and Beyond">Modern Mathematicians</a>, Noether was the only woman represented among the notable mathematicians of the modern world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Noether has been honored in several memorials,
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Women_in_Mathematics" title="Association for Women in Mathematics">Association for Women in Mathematics</a> holds a <a href="/wiki/Noether_Lecture" title="Noether Lecture">Noether Lecture</a> to honor women in mathematics every year; in its 2005 pamphlet for the event, the Association characterizes Noether as "one of the great mathematicians of her time, someone who worked and struggled for what she loved and believed in. Her life and work remain a tremendous inspiration".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[138]</a></sup></li>
<li>Consistent with her dedication to her students, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Siegen" title="University of Siegen">University of Siegen</a> houses its mathematics and physics departments in buildings on <i>the Emmy Noether Campus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li>The German Research Foundation (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deutsche_Forschungsgemeinschaft" title="Deutsche Forschungsgemeinschaft">Deutsche Forschungsgemeinschaft</a>) operates the <i>Emmy Noether Programme</i>, providing funding to early-career researchers to rapidly qualify for a leading position in science and research by leading an independent junior research group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[140]</a></sup></li>
<li>A street in her hometown, Erlangen, has been named after Emmy Noether and her father, Max Noether.</li>
<li>The successor to the secondary school she attended in Erlangen has been renamed as <i>the Emmy Noether School</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g_noether_p167_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-g_noether_p167-138">[135]</a></sup></li>
<li>A series of high school workshops and competitions are held in her honor in May of each year since 2001, originally hosted by a subsequent woman mathematics <a href="/wiki/Privatdozent" title="Privatdozent">Privatdozent</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_G%C3%B6ttingen" title="University of Göttingen">University of Göttingen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[141]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Perimeter_Institute_for_Theoretical_Physics" title="Perimeter Institute for Theoretical Physics">Perimeter Institute for Theoretical Physics</a> annually awards Emmy Noether Visiting Fellowships<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[142]</a></sup> to outstanding female theoretical physicists. Perimeter Institute is also home to the Emmy Noether Council,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[143]</a></sup> a group of volunteers made up of international community, corporate and philanthropic leaders work together to increase the number of women in physics and mathematical physics at Perimeter Institute.</li>
<li>The Emmy Noether Mathematics Institute in Algebra, Geometry and Function Theory in the Department of Mathematics and Computer Science, <a href="/wiki/Bar-Ilan_University" title="Bar-Ilan University">Bar-Ilan University</a>, Ramat Gan, Israel was jointly founded in 1992 by the university, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_government" title="German government">German government</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Minerva_Foundation" title="Minerva Foundation">Minerva Foundation</a> with the aim to stimulate research in the above fields and to encourage collaborations with Germany. Its main topics are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algebraic_Geometry" title="Algebraic Geometry">Algebraic Geometry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Group_theory" title="Group theory">Group theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">Complex Function Theory</a>. Its activities includes local research projects, conferences, short-term visitors, post-doc fellowships, and the Emmy Noether lectures (an annual series of distinguished lectures). ENI is a member of ERCOM: "European Research Centers of Mathematics".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[144]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2013, The European Physical Society established the Emmy Noether Distinction for Women in Physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[145]</a></sup> Winners have included Dr <a href="/wiki/Catalina_Curceanu" title="Catalina Curceanu">Catalina Curceanu</a>, Prof <a href="/wiki/Sibylle_G%C3%BCnter" title="Sibylle Günter">Sibylle Günter</a> and Prof <a href="/wiki/Anne_L%27Huillier" title="Anne L'Huillier">Anne L'Huillier</a>.</li></ul>
<p>In fiction, Emmy Nutter, the physics professor in "The God Patent" by <a href="/wiki/Ransom_Stephens" title="Ransom Stephens">Ransom Stephens</a>, is based on Emmy Noether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Farther from home,
</p>
<ul><li>The crater <a href="/wiki/N%C3%B6ther_(crater)" title="Nöther (crater)">Nöther</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon" title="Far side of the Moon">far side of the Moon</a> is named after her.</li>
<li>The minor planet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/7001_Noether" title="7001 Noether">7001 Noether</a> is named for Emmy Noether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmadel2003570_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmadel2003570-150">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[148]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> put a memorial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_doodle" title="Google doodle">doodle</a> created by Google artist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sophie_Diao&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sophie Diao (page does not exist)">Sophie Diao</a> on Google's homepage in many countries on 23 March 2015 to celebrate Emmy Noether's 133rd birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[149]</a></sup></li>
<li>On 6 November 2020, a satellite named after her (<a href="/wiki/%C3%91uSat" title="ÑuSat">ÑuSat 13</a> or "Emmy", COSPAR 2020-079E) was launched into space.</li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Emmy_Noether&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Rufname-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Rufname_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Rufname_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Emmy_(given_name)" title="Emmy (given name)">Emmy</a> is the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rufname" title="Rufname">Rufname</a></i>, the second of two official given names, intended for daily use. Cf. for example the résumé submitted by Noether to Erlangen University in 1907 (Erlangen University archive, <i>Promotionsakt Emmy Noether</i> (1907/08, NR. 2988); reproduced in: <i>Emmy Noether, Gesammelte Abhandlungen – Collected Papers,</i> ed. N. Jacobson 1983; online facsimile at <a class="external text" href="http://www.physikerinnen.de/noetherlebenslauf.html" rel="nofollow">physikerinnen.de/noetherlebenslauf.html</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070929100418/http://www.physikerinnen.de/noetherlebenslauf.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 29 September 2007 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>). Sometimes <i>Emmy</i> is mistakenly reported as a short form for <i>Amalie</i>, or misreported as "Emily". e.g. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1067248974">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit;word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation q{quotes:"\"""\"""'""'"}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}.mw-parser-output title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Edge&amp;rft.atitle=Special+Relativity+%E2%80%93+Why+Can%27t+You+Go+Faster+Than+Light%3F&amp;rft.aulast=Smolin&amp;rft.aufirst=Lee&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.edge.org%2Fdocuments%2Farchive%2Fedge52.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEmmy+Noether"></span></span>
</li>
